RATING: 9
A lot of fun, we've played this with several groups of friends and everyone has enjoyed it. The variety of map layouts and treasure clues allow each game to play out differently, and the production quality is great (particularly the map and the idols). Although it's quite simple and easy to pick up, there's also more depth than might appear at first glance (e.g., the order in which clues are played against a given treasure determines the bidding order for the treasure cards, and the amulets scattered across the island can be used in a variety of ways). Highly recommended!

RATING: 6.5
On the complexity level, we're in the same realms as Takenoko. It's family-friendly, non-gamer friendly, girlfriend-friendly stuff. A beautiful board and components, a nice theme, and good simple mechanics. This isn't taxing but it is fun. There are decisions to be made so it isn't all luck. There are opportunities to interfere with your opponents' plans, but it's never an aggressive game. 

Traded after two plays - decisions were too simple for my taste. Received Thurn und Taxis (with two expansions) in return -  a fantastic deal!

RATING: 5.5
Surprisingly complex for such a fun theme, so I don't see this as an easy gateway style family game at all. The clues for locating treasure is original and fun, but is a bit of a brain burner at the start (especially so when you can't quite put marking cubes out). The ATV movement is also more complex than needed. Still, fun theme, richly produced with a creative variable board set up. In sum: it's alright, but I'm not sure how many plays it will have with my game group and it's likely too complex for family play. Probably going on the trade pile.

RATING: 6
Lives in the niche of heavy family games, especially with the expert rules. Basically there is only a choice between two possible actions on each turn. However, there is a zillion things you can do with the amulets. It is hard to memorize them all.

Tobago look good and plays rather well. At some points you might experience analysis paralysis to a certain degree but it's not because of the myriad of choices rather than because of trying to figure out whether you have any legal moves.

I have a feeling that the rating might rise once I get more plays under my belt so that I can fully concentrate on tactics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A clever and altogether unique game with excellent components and a elegant design. Tobago is a deductive game unlike anything I have seen before. You play a group of treasure hunters (who drive around in little SUV meeples!) who are (sort of) working together just enough to deduce where there is treasure on the island you are exploring. The more each member contributes to deducing a treasures location, the larger their share of treasure will be when it is ultimately discovered. Beware the cursed treasure though, as it can really ruin a treasure hunters day. The island is also littered with plenty of amulets (delivered by cool stone easter island looking staue components!) that you can use for different things to benefit you on your search. The game is not difficult to learn or teach. There are few rules and the mechanics are simple. Definitely a OOP game worth adding to your collection.

RATING: 8
FAVOURITE GAME - Tobago is a treasuring hunting game where players use their and their opponents clues to find a treasures exact location. Some of treasures are cursed and only the help of the Amulets will protect them. But these amulets have other magical powers as well. The player with the most gold will win the game! The beautifully illustrated game board and top-quality game pieces enhance the allure and mystery of this inventive and exhilarating game. Each player contributes valuable clues which determine where the treasures are located. The excitement mounts as the adventurers strive to be the first to arrive at the treasure sites. The 3-piece game board with its innovative design can be set up with 32 different scenarios, each providing fun and a new challenge.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
1st play: Great production, mid-weight logic/deduction game that plays quickly and encourages team-work through shared objectives.  Sometimes, the best plays might be to throw down a clue that doesn't really help solve the treasure hunt but gets you into the splitting of the loot.  Pulling the curses is brutal, and can make the game swingy, but it's short enough that I don't mind.  I do think the logic portion may push it out of family-game range, but a nice fast game that's not like other games I own.

2nd play: Plays in under an hour which makes it a nice quick game.  The main problem this game has is that the logic puzzles are slightly too complex for non-gamer types to enjoy, and not quite complex enough for gamer types.  The treasures are fairly random, which is ok given the length.  I still like the production and I can see pulling this out at the end of the night as a group pick.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.7
A very, VERY strange game. There is just SOOOO much to take into consideration when making your move, but it really is just a deep version of a push-your-luck game. Weird. And (believe it or not) there IS the potential for analysis paralysis in it! The tension level is very high and constant (except for the first few turns, perhaps), and all kinds of games of psychological brinksmanship are going on between the players. It is highly interactive, and uniquely "enforced cooperative": it doesn't matter what action you choose to take, it will benefit somebody else in some way! I really like that. Tobago is one of the best themed games I have ever played....so well themed that it really needs a soundtrack. 
The best treasure hunting game I have ever played!
AND, on a relatively minor note, the plastic insert is actually quite useful! EVERYTHING fits in perfectly, and the board sections keep everything from jumping out of place.

RATING: 7.5
Most deduction games are all about who-dun-its or some such silliness.  This game takes the classic deduction concept and uses it as a mid-stream mechanism.  Reaching the answer is not the end of the story.  That fact makes this design quite interesting.  A familiar concept is leveraged in a new way.  Combine the ideas with very well made components, clear rules, and you have a title that really should be highly approachable to many families.  Easily should have made it into the SdJ finalists for 2009.  No clue why it didn't.

RATING: 8
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Spiel des Jahres Recommendation 2010[/SIZE][/COLOR]

[i]Posted: 6/29/10, Rated: 8[/i]

In an age of [thing=36128]deck building[/thing], I was very curious to see how a game could implement a "build a treasure map" mechanic.  How would [i]Tobago[/i] provide multiple puzzles to solve if they were being determined as the game progressed?  I had to find out.  More importantly, I thought the idea was cool.

That's an important detail: I really liked the idea of this game before I ever opened it.  I started with a positive bias.

As a result, I was fascinated when I saw how the board can be assembled in theoretically 32 different ways.  The iconology for the clues, and the method of constructing the "treasure maps" are brilliantly simple.  The moai-esque statue figures are fantastic.  The ATV movement feels very similar to the token movement in [i][thing=855]Java[/thing][/i].  The treasure "auctions" are a novel way to distribute points, and players have to balance their contributions to each treasure.

The artwork is highly saturated, and pleasing.  I was immediately reminded of two other [company=87]Zoch Verlag[/company] games: [i][thing=15817]Manila[/thing][/i], and [i][thing=13308]Niagara[/thing][/i]; [person=12587]Victor Boden[/person] was the artist for all three titles.  I am a fan of his work.

With a few tweaks, [i]Tobago[/i] could be a "9:" first, treasure cards which are not revealed should be discarded without looking at them.  If this means a curse card gets into the discard deck, then so be it.  Second, unused amulets ought to have some point value, otherwise the whole amulet mechanism feels bolted-on.  As is, [i]Tobago[/i] is an enjoyable discovery with a novel concept.  It can easily be taught to new players, and that is a treasure indeed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Probably one of the most unusual and unique games I've ever come across.  It seems that the bulk of the intrigue surrounding this game comes down to the induction process where players are trying to narrow down where the treasures might be.  The variable setup of the board is very cool as well, and the components are top notch.

I think the only real downside to the game is that the rules are somewhat counter-intuitive.  Non-gamers will not catch on right away, and in some cases it may take more than one full game for them to catch up.  The nice thing is that players can work together a little bit because the play of one player's cards doesn't necessarily affect the play of another player.  With some hand-holding I think this game could be a real gem, and I may at some point decide to pick up a copy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game and Lost Valley are my all-time favorite family games. Your choices in Tobago are really wide open and there are opportunities for cleaver play. It isn't super deep but it is fun and it looks great.  

Update:
I've played 10-12 games now. I thought I would lose interest in this one but I have grown to like it more. Raised it from an 8 to 8.5. I'm tempted to rate it a 9 but the game is just so darn simple and there is a good deal of luck.

The tactics are subtle but they are there. You do your best to nudge each treasure towards your side of the island. You set yourself up to grab amulets.  You try and get the most out of every turn. Each turn you have a hand full of decent options. The trick is figuring out which of those options is slightly better for you and if, perhaps, there is a super clever move hidden within your options (hey, look at that. I just totally stole that treasure from you). I’m curious to see how I will feel after another 10 plays.

More than anything this game feels unique and for me that's a big deal. 

Update: I've played at least 20 games now and I still really enjoy the game. It's not super deep but I always have fun playing it. We play with the variant that lets you place 2 compass roses when you dig up a treasure (we don't place a rose on initial clue cards to keep game length the same). This variant makes claiming treasures much more important.

I'm a bit embarrassed but I have to raise this one to a "9". It is really just a family game but it is so thematic and clever I just have to give it a top score. It is a game I like to pull out to show people that board games can actually be a really cool way to spend an evening. It is a fantastic gateway game!

RATING: 6
Interesting, somewhat unique.

I like chaotic, random games, but this has a weird mix.  Everything comes down to the treasure cards that range from (if memory serves) 1 to 6 gold.  That's far too varied a result and has far more effect on who wins than practically any decisions the player makes.  I feel the game would be better of the range was more like 5 to 10 maybe, so the swings aren't as ridiculous.

This problem is exacerbated when played with only two players.  When it's easy for treasures to be finished completely by one player, the results are [u]far[/u] too varied -- when one player gets a curse, the other gets 6,6,5, what can you do?  I strongly discourage play with just two.

Again, I like family games, and I like luck-driven, chaotic games, but I don't like this one.  At least it's pretty.

RATING: 8
I don't know if Tobago is worth the heaps of praise that have been thrown upon it lately, but it certainly is good. Like most of the best gateway/family euros, there are only a couple of options per turn (drive your atv, or play a treasure map card), but each option opens up a slew of possible actions (which card to play and which treasure to play it on?; where to drive?).
My only issue with Tobago is that it might be a bit too simple for serious gamers, and a bit too difficult for gateway gamers.  
Hit the table 3 times in 3 days so far, which says a lot in a collection that receives between 2 - 10 new games a week (Hey! That's my job!)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Pretty cool little game of pin-pointing treasure locations and driving your truck around to "claim" them!  I really enjoyed the process of narrowing down the location of the treasures, and trying to get the final treasure position near my truck location in order to get to it first.  The other cool thing is that everyone that contributed information as to the whereabouts of the treasure gets a cut even if they didn't actually "claim" it with their truck; they just get less control over how good their cut of the treasure is.

At first blush, the game seems fairly chaotic, as anybody can lay map sections for any treasure, resulting in little control over where the treasures end up on the map.  However, the amulets are key to having control over the proceedings.  Since amulet locations are predictable, you can maneuver your truck in order to give yourself the best chance of claiming amulets.  Amulets basically allow you to take multiple turns at a time, which is key to both pinpointing and claiming a treasure.  So, to me, the key to the game really is having amulets on hand.

Overall, just a really cool game that I really look forward playing more.  Plus, it's light enough that I'll be able to play it with the family!  Good stuff!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I like it! Simple but also very engaging. Turns are short which keeps the game going at a nice pace. The theme, bits, and short ruleset may make this a good game for non-gamers (you may want to omit the amulets in this instance, however.)

The treasure finding system reminds me of Old Town, but I find these games scratch different itches. In Old Town, the satisfaction comes from having one overall puzzle to solve broken into small chunks which makes for a nice sense of progression, and, when done, conquering a large puzzle. In this game, the satisfaction comes from trying to solve as many small, bite-sized puzzles as you can giving it a simplified, lighter feel.

The game has a nice (but not overwhelming) amount of tension and strategy as you try to keep up with the clue cards other players are playing - making sure you're in on each treasure chest and timing your clues so you get as close to first dibs as possible is essential. The press-your-luck element that comes into play during treasure distribution is nice too - just be careful not to pass TOO much!

RATING: 5
Intitial rating an 8. First off, this is probably the best looking game in my collection. The components and board are drop-dead gorgeous. I can see why some are calling this a contender for Spiel de Jahres.

After one play, it strikes me as a polite game. There's not a lot of conflict, everyone gets some treasure, everyone can walk away with some mini victories. The strategies are still a little obtuse to me, but I look forward to playing this more. Although the turn supposedly consists of "play a card or move your jeep," in fact the raising of treasures, the use of amulets actually adds some complexity... more than you would expect in a family game.

Update: a two-player game felt a little flat. Not sure if this is designed for two or not. More plays should reveal if the gameplay changes much with the number of players, or if I'm just a bit cold on the game as a whole.

Update: Traded away along with Acquire and Incan Gold for Goa. Man, I guess "mechanics mattter." This was hard to part with, as it was easily the most visually striking game I owned. The game is flat-out gorgeous. But, I could just never get past the fact that collecting treasures were largely inconsequential when everyone shares in the booty. It's too complex for a family game, and yet it lacks enough "meat" or penalizing systems to be a "gamer's game." I love that process-of-elimination mechanic... it's just the rest that bugged me.

Let's hope my friends like Goa instead...

RATING: 7
Map making where you "create the truth", establishing where the treasure is. Really nice mechanic.

Simplicity with a layered approach; establish the location, move to location, dig it up and distribute the gold. Contributing to the location gets you a share in the treasure, regardless of who digs it up. Really helps with player interaction.

The mini-game of distributing the treasure also helps mix things up, keeps the pacing fresh.

The amulets seem a little tacked on for the sake of "a little something extra", but the game could do without them. Maybe I wont be saying that after a few more plays.

Nice components over all with a uniquely shaped and modular board.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I both understand and do not understand the praise this title is getting. I understand its appeal: digging for treasure on an island using partial clues is a widely-appealing subject. The author did a good job representing this aspect, and it's always kinda neat to see how the number of options shrinks to one, subsequently followed by digging up the treasures. 

But in terms of [i]game[/i], especially [i]interesting game[/i] for the spoilt gamer... No. Once you've dug up one treasure you've dug them all up. There is no progression in this game; just a long string of card additions to a number of cubes, ever shrinking the number of options. You don't get better at finding treasure; you don't get worse either. Tobago turns into quintessential family material where the activity of doing something together is as important as the activity itself, let alone that the activity is a battle of minds. I see Tobago more as [i]activity[/i] than as [i]game[/i], and that means my interest in this game is very fleeting. If the treasure hunt was more involved, and had players thinking about various consequences in dealing with the hunt, then the game might have been worthwhile for me. As it stands now, it isn't.

But still: if you are looking for a nice family game you can put in front of people who hardly ever play... Then Tobago is certainly not a bad choice to make.

RATING: 6
Components: Gorgeous board. Great wooden components. Good iconography.

The game needs a cheatsheet to show what a player may do with an amulet. Also the rules are a bit fiddley re: not using an amulet to get another amulet, since usually a player can just use the regular part of their turn to get the amulet, then use the amulet to go to something else (having the effect that the player just removed the amulet from circulation).

Experience: With just two players, it seems too laborious to both provide clues and drive to pick up treasure. With three players, it plays much better; treasures are quickly located, and I find myself pressured to have at least a part in constructing each of the treasure maps.

I like the logic aspect of trying to apply layers of clues that will place treasure nearer to me, but it almost seems more important to be nearer hotspots of amulets after the treasure has been raised.

First Played: 2012-03-10
Last Played: 2013-01-05
Eagerness to Play Again: Within a year

Plays (2):
2 players: 1
3 players: 1

RATING: 5
Tobago has a great set of mechanics, but its flavor is a little bit weak. I very much enjoy the logic puzzle of placing clues to search for treasure, and the amulets add quite a bit of interesting strategy as well. It is a little slow to start, and that is one of my few complaints, but really takes off after the first treasure has been found. The art and figures are also fantastic.

Downsides include the relation of flavor to mechanics. It always seems like we are speculating about the treasure, "I think the treasure is on a mountain" etc, and then it appears where it is 'rumored' to be. I also think the curses are either too much or too little, in that having only two of them can remove a lot of tension late game.

RATING: N/A
$57 Amazon
Tobago is an adventure game, in which the players possess different parts of treasure maps. During the game, more and more information about the locations of the treasures are revealed, and the possible locations are narrowed down. When a player identifies the location of one of the treasures, they try to reach it as fast as possible to secure the findings.

RATING: 8.1
This may not be a top game but is so good in so many ways.

First there is the "backward deduction" mechanism to find the treasures, that is really cool, then there is the hand menagement combined with the board movement to be at the right place at the right time, then there is the search for the right time to use the amulets and, at last, the press your luck/odds mechanism to share each treasure loot...
The artwork and the theme are great, the components are of good quality and the modular board ensures new play every time. The game suits families, boardgamers and even, sometimes, parties. What more can you ask from a game?

RATING: 8.5
Treasure hunting game with a modular island map, plastic palm trees and stone idols. You play treasure map cards as clues to eliminate possible treasure locations, then when you've found one, go and get it in your ATV. RATING: 7
Bon jeu au matériel splendide.
Plusieurs parties sont nécessaires pour bien intégrer les règles qui doivent absolument être complétées par la FAQ officielle Gigamic.
Ce que j'ai fait en créant un ficher PDF des règles intégrant directement la FAQ, tout en conservant la superbe mise en page d'origine ! 
Je tiens mes règles à disposition via message personnel. 

RQ : autant pour le Cluedo 2008 j'ai fait travail de réécriture, autant pour Tobago j'ai juste complété avec les infos officielles pour faire des règles béton ! 
J'espère que vous aurez ainsi autant de plaisir que moi à y jouer. 

NB : au début, il n'est pas évident de bien avoir en tête l'organigramme des actions possibles lors d'un tour de jeu et des combos (genre action supplémentaire puis action de base puis action supplémentaire puis récupérations de plusieurs trésors de suite !). Cela prend plusieurs parties pour bien maîtriser les règles et donc les parties sont plutôt longues au début, même avec une petite carte (plateau de jeu côté "lettres minuscules"). Patience !

On peut y jouer en ligne :

RATING: 8
Players are treasure hunters driving their 4WD ATV's all over the island of Tobago, searching for treasures.  Simple to play game, where players on their turn either move their vehicle over the hexmap modular board, or play a clue card against a treasure.  There are four treasures to find at any one time, and clues gradually refine the location of these treasures until only one hex remains - the location.  A player who moves their ATV to the location may raise that treasure, and all clue contributors are entitled to a portion.  Nice push your luck rules for sharing the treasure means you may get a low value card, or a curse!  Lovely looking game, plays well and is deceptively deep.  Millions of board setups (32 different boards, and the idol, tree and hut pieces can be set up anywhere) - no game will ever play the same way twice.  Great friends game, or one for families with older children.  Very good.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players compete to discover buried treasure using a novel process-of-elimination card system.  Each player who helps to narrow down the location of the treasure gets a share, although I am not enthusiastic about how it gets distributed.  Also, the curse cards, though thematic, somehow seem out of place.  The treasure-hunting mechanism itself is very novel, but it seems like the scoring system was an afterthought and isn't the greatest.  The game includes a couple of variants that I have yet to try.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
My first impression so far is that the game is definitely interesting and beautiful, and the mechanics are quite neat and unique.  These are all factors that are important to me in a game, however the actual gameplay is not quite as fun as I would like. It seems to drag at times but this could just be due to slow play as we learn the game. It's still enjoyable though and is definitely unlike any other game in my collection. RATING: 6.63
Adventure, family game where 4 ATVs are racing around the tropical island of Tobago to find as much buried treasure as they can. Every player contributes to the maps of 4 different treasure chests by reducing the possible location for it, until the location is reduced to one possible field. And then digging and distribution of treasure commences. There are also mysterious statues that leave magic amulets around and there are curses in the treasure chests. It's fun racing game of chances with smart mechanic of map making.

[b]Update:[/b] Rating modified according to a custom rating system.

[b]Favorite mechanism:[/b] Pinpointing the treasure location by adding the clues to the map and reducing the possible locations.

[b][thread=487260]The PLAYMOBIL® Legion reviews "Tobago"[/thread][/b]

(As of [b]5[/b] plays) A family game with gorgeous components. Manages to downplay direct player confrontation while still maintaining a competitive atmosphere. RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I think Tobago's mismarketed. It isn't a deduction game as the box states, since any clues played are visible by all of the players. And if that isn't enough, there are cubes placed to ensure that everyone knows exactly where the treasure is eventually going to be. It's a crutch that I'm not sure is necessary, and it takes any deduction out of the game. All you have to figure out is a) which of your cards will narrow down the treasure's location and b) how quickly you can get your vehicle to pick it up. There is a fair amount of tactical play as the game continues, but there isn't much long-term planning. You spend much of your time reacting to other players instead. That's not necessarily a bad thing, but it is much different than what you might expect.

Tobago is a good family game, but I don't think there's much replay value -- even with the different islands you can make. The way you assemble them is ingenious, but the gameplay just isn't there to support all of the great components. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]Rating based on - 25+ plays, 2 player - 3 player - 4 player
Preferred player Count: 3 or 4[/b]
I was initially interested in this game because of the name and could not find a copy for months so eventually I learned and played my first few games on Board Game Arena. I think more players make it more exciting. I eventually got a copy in trade and it has only been played by 2 which can certainly be more strategic but it not half as exciting. This game uses a mechanism that we do not have in any of our other games and for that reason alone it is a keeper. You are given a handful of clue cards and you use the clues to pinpoint (or in actuality determine) where the island treasures are located. Treasure hunting is kind of an instabuy for me but also I like the exploration/deduction aspect that accompanies it. Technically you can just sit in one spot or move to convenient spots and not do very much the entire game, but I avoid players who work the angle of a game or try to break the game. That sort of play style makes games boring for me. I believe games should be played in the spirit meant which is 'exploration and treasure hunting'. Does not hurt that I got married in Tobago and am planning to move to Tobago in a few years :) Will definitely have a place on my shelf there. The artwork and components are stunning, and this game has the BEST INSERT EVER!!!

RATING: 4.5
Really quick and a lot simpler than I expected. I may even be able to play this with my daughter (8).

Actually I am a little disappointed with how simple this is. I was expecting it to take 4-5 clues to narrow down a treasure sufficiently however usually a pair of clues is sufficient although I do believe set up has more of an influence upon this than first suspected.

The amulets are too easy to come by therefore you can effectively monopolise a treasure by ensuring you discover it all before on opponent gets to play any cards.

The two curse cards are really insignificant. Although they are devastating if and when they come up once the two are gone you can play risk free. 

I think the player claiming the final treasure card getting to place the next clue and get a compass rose upon it is very very powerful and I think we shall house rule that the top card of the deck is turned instead.

Traded in a math trade (2014) for Gem Dealer.

RATING: 7
Tobago
Rules Complexity: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Strategic Depth: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Luck Factor :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
Negotiation Factor: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime: :star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Component Quality: :star::star::star::star::star:
Player Interaction: :star::halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Theme Integration: :star::star::star::star::nostar:

It's been called a deduction game, but it's more about making the deduction puzzle than solving it, so it doesn't require nearly as much brain matter as I first thought it might.  This is an extremely well produced game that plays in under an hour and is great for families and adults that want something lighter.  Luck plays a significant role in what clue cards come up, and especially which treasure cards appear.  Treasure is distributed Saboteur-style, but with the added element of 2 possible curse cards, which add a bit of tension and opportunity for bluffing.

In short, it's a poor ending, but a fun journey.

RATING: 5
Tobago may be very close to the quintessential family game. It's got an exciting theme (treasure hunt!), gorgeous components, light strategy and a good dose of plain old luck. So much so, in fact, that I doubt the best player will win this with anything approaching consistency.

The way players zero in on the treasure locations by playing clue cards is pretty clever and is both the heart and the soul of the game, but it also has a few elements that seem to be there purely for thematic reasons as they don't really add anything to the gameplay (especially the truck movement).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
"Tobago" a été une vraie claque ludique. La première partie m'a bluffé, tant j'avais l'impression d'avoir joué à quelque chose d'unique. Et puis immédiatement, le plaisir ludique était très présent, durant toute la partie. J'avais l'impression de retourner en enfance au cours de l'une des chasses au trésor organisé par mes parents pour mes anniversaires (effet madeleine de Proust).

Ce qui fait tout le sel du jeu, c'est vraiment ce mécanisme original de recherche du trésor et de la restriction des possibilités. Chacun cherche à se placer un peu partout au mieux, tout en essayant, si possible, de prendre ce trésor si convoité pour avoir la possibilité de choisir en premier. Le mécanisme de recherche de trésor est presque coopératif, et particulièrement bien pensé, même si parfois un peu brise-neurones (est-ce que ça fait avancer les choses, comment je peux restreindre les recherches ?). A travers ce mécanisme se met en place toute la subtilité du jeu : se placer un peu partout, essayer d'avoir le plus de roses des vents, réussir à attraper le trésor et donc dévoiler le trésor au moment opportun, être placé le "plus bas" pour pouvoir choisir avant les autres et éviter les malédictions...

La répartition des trésors est également bien pensée, bien que moins originale et dont le risque est toutefois assez faible, le jeu ne comportant que deux malédictions qui, finalement, ne renversent tout de même pas le jeu (on craint beaucoup plus les "Epidémies" de "Pandémie", par exemple), même s'il est assez jouissif de réussir à éviter une malédiction tout en profitant de la répartition d'un trésor. Toutefois, la part de hasard (on ne connaît pas toutes les cartes, sauf exception) compensée par des probabilités et le mécanisme reposant sur l'équilibre risque/prudence fonctionne bien et met un peu de piquant lors de cette phase.

Les amulettes, elles, se révèlent également indispensables. Pour éviter les malédictions, certes, mais aussi pour pleinement saisir une opportunité à un moment donné, permettant une liberté d'action bien plus grande, ce qui s'avère nécessaire au fil du jeu, on ne peut plus se contenter des deux actions de départ.

Mais malgré toute cette richesse qui rend le jeu intéressant pour les joueurs réguliers, le jeu est tout à fait abordable pour des joueurs novices. Le thème est bien présent (sauf pour les voitures qui vont sur l'eau), immersif (on se sent à la recherche du trésor, nous promenant sur l'île, à travers les différents paysages... ça facilite l'entrée dans le jeu. De plus, les mécanismes, bien que non dénués de subtilité, sont simples et fluides, rapides à mettre en place (partie rapide), non sans écarter une certaine réflexion.

Ajoutez à cela un matériel de qualité, un visuel attractif, un mécanisme de répartition des amulettes par les statues très ludique (même si les prmeières parties sont un peu désorganisées : "Mais tu l'as déjà bougé elle, non, vu que y'a l'amulette ici..."). Bref, pour moi, "Tobago" est l'une des plus belles sorties ludiques de ces dernières années, qui brille tant par son originalité mécanique que par un thème immersif. Une très bonne porte d'entrée dans le monde ludique moderne, et néanmoins un plaisir intact et renouvelé pour des joueurs plus expérimentés. Une chasse au trésor que l'on n'oublie pas, ça non. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Bought the game due to it's awesome components, however I had certain gripes about the game where the components could not make up for it.

1) Confusion over certain actions such as:
Picking up amulets - at the start of your turn and you are on a space occupying an amulet/completing a movement leg.
For new players, they get the impression that you have to move your ATV to get an amulet where you can actually pick an amulet up if your ATV started the turn on top of an amulet and then u can play a clue card.

2) The requirement for extreme spatial visualization in some cases:
When you have a clue card which states within a terrain feature, it makes it much easier to narrow down treasures, however when you start playing clues such as not within a terrain, not next to a statue, not within sight of a hut, not next to a tree... trying to find out which hexes could contain the treasure takes quite awhile (at least for those I play with).

3) The judging of number of hexes away... I ended up just visualizing two circles from the landmark in order to estimate those clue cards containing "within sight of"

Personal gripes... so may or may not happen when you play it.

RATING: 6
Tobago is a beautiful looking game and one that doesn't play like anything I know. The 'forced deduction' mechanic is unique and works for what it is. It runs a bit dry at times, but for the length and easy complexity it doesn't cause too much of an issue. That being said, I think I've played this enough times to realize its shortcomings.  Most of the time on a turn a player's move is either blindly apparent or somewhat arbitrary. As such, little decision making is present. The game comes off as a blunt, tactical procedure, and what little fun that comes out of it is robbed by the random nature of the cards and the Treasure system. It's a shame, as the 'forced deduction' is certainly interesting. I only wish there was a better game here.

RATING: 7
This game is beautiful. The components are very well made and look great. It is a sort of semi deduction game where all players are playing their hint cards to add clues to each treasure map. Then once narrowed down to one space. Everyone races their jeeps to try and be the first one to dig up the treasure. Digging it up gives you a bonus when the treasure is divided amongst the players. RATING: 10
Adventure = Must Buy

I'm such a sucker for adventure and nice bits and this one delivers on both fronts. First of all, it's looks amazing.

Secondly, it's actually a very deep game, with hard decisions all the time. There's lots of strategy in this game, and the luck is mitigated very very well.

I have to commend the designer on this one; a very good, complex and deep euro with such simple and easy to grasp mechanics which offers something other games in this genre don't; awesome theme. The theme doesn't feel tacked on, which is rare for a euro. It feels as though that Tobago is a euro that was designed for ameritrash fans like myself, and to suck me into deeper thinking man's euro games. It's light, but has some really good think-ey parts to it to keep the heavy gamers occupied, and is easy enough for lighter gamers too. It hits a nice balance of everything, and is just lots and lots of fun.

Some games with lots of thinking, and deep gameplay don't really grab me that often, not to mention so very few euros that are able to do this over the ameritrash luck-fests I'm a huge fan of.

Tobago is an amazing game that I'd recommend for anyone. It might be a bit much for family gaming, but it's tough to say, since the theme and bits are so appealing. I feel it might be the number of options available that might confuse and confound the non-gamer's out there, but I could be wrong, and haven't tested the waters with non-gamers just yet.

Tobago is an excellent choice for those families that are ready to move on from Ticket to Ride, Carcasonne and Settlers, and are looking for something a bit deeper, and more complex. The look and feel of this game will grab nearly anyone's attention, and once they play it, they'll want to keep bringing it out.

The Lady and I played it three times the first time we opened the box, and Galaxy trucker is the only other game that has done this...ever.

One of the very few euros we enjoy, and of course, a lot of the folk on BGG deem this to be mediocre at best. If you want a euro that isn't dry, bland or boring, then get Tobago.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Tobago is a fun, friendly, attractive family-weight game that has the feel of a potential Spiel des Jahres winner - but it wasn't even nominated.  Players play cards that gradually winnow possible locations for a number of different buried treasures.  Every time a player plays one of these cards, they're contributing to the search for that treasure, and will be rewarded with one of the treasure cards that will be drawn when it is found.  The player who drives to the location and physically finds the treasure will also get a card - likely the best one.  The game generates a satisfying cooperative feeling.  Players are not on the same team, but whenever a card is played they will work together to deduce the possible locations of a treasure and mark them with cubes on the board.  This "thinking together" gives the game a friendly feeling that I think most players will enjoy, and creates a lot of pleasant social interaction.

Be warned: this is a very random game in ways that will frustrate players who are concerned with fairness and equitable game balance.  It's very possible to end up with a hand of cards that prevents you from doing anything useful.  Treasures might pop up within the reach of your truck, or they might all be found on the other end of the board.  There is heaps and heaps of randomness to the treasure card collection, especially surrounding the two curse cards - which can really screw you even if you have the protection of an amulet. Honestly, this is the sort of game that is most enjoyable if players don't put too much thought into optimizing every play they make.

Those who have played Old Town will recognize some very similar concepts in Tobago.  Although it's not as puzzly-thinky or difficult as Old Town, I think Tobago fits together better and is much more enjoyable.  Even for someone like me who tends to go for the puzzly-thinky choice in most cases.

RATING: 7
Tobago is an excellent game.  There is a lot of player interaction yet it never feels tense because of it.  EXCEPT when it comes to taking treasure cards, "I hope they take that four so I get my six."

It is fiddly though, and some rules are easy to over look.  I have played at least 10 games of this and I still forget to put out the amulets.  

I also feel that it would benefit from more curse cards or, even better, an unknown quantity of them.  These add a lot of (good) tension and the game gets a little stale when player's know there will be no more curses because we've seen two.

RATING: 6.5
Excellent artwork and production values.  Treasure map making mechanic is almost like bidding and is quite clever and unique.  Advanced amulet powers make a good game better.  

Game has (to me) somewhat of a beginning, mid, and end game feel due to the presence of two curse cards in the treasure deck; you know that the first 12 treasure cards cannot have a curse in there.  After the 12th treasure is issued, we play treasure maps much more cautiously due to the possibility of getting a curse (mitigated by having an amulet); however, after the two curse cards are gone, we try to make the treasure maps as long as possible, which means more treasure for you when it's found.

My last session of Tobago seemed to draaag, and somewhat soured my view of this game.  I have a soft spot for this game but I traded it for LOTR: Confrontation, which was an excellent trade because my wife enjoys LOTR much more than Tobago.

RATING: 5
I give it a 7 as a lighter, family, "gateway" game OR for gamers that thrill over the excellent and evocative buried treasure theme. For more experienced or hardcore gamers, I'd give it a 6 based on gameplay. 

Much of it is mitigated random, and the two curse cards in the treasure deck don't do enough to sway gameflow. Getting rid of them outright (which is suggested to tweak the game in the rulebook) might be a variant to employ before your first game.  Amulets have more than one power, so even getting rid of the curse cards, players would probably not alter their strategy too much.

My copy is opened, complete, and has been played twice.

RATING: 6
I've played this game once so far after only half-listening to the rules, but I got the gist of it.  It might be worth playing again, but it's not a great game overall.

I find it a bit chaotic, because you might have a great plan for a future use of a particular card, but someone else might play another card on a treasure map that makes that card useless.  There is a lot of luck-of-the-draw for cards, which isn't necessarily a deal breaker -- I'd have to play more to decide.

The amulets and curses are good fun, and throw some extra strategic considerations into the mix.

This is a game that would be much more fun with computer assistance, to help everybody answer the question "what would happen if I played this card on this treasure map".  There are 16 such calculations to be made by each player on each turn (4 cards x 4 treasures).  People can often make mistakes even when all players are watching the board, so I'd say that that's the clunkiest part of the game.

Interesting rules question that came up: if there happen to be 2 treasures (single cubes) in the same hex, can a single ATV pick it up with one action?  We played that you could only get one treasure per ATV move.

RATING: 6.5
Interesting game with treasure/points collection based on a series of logical strictures, added by the players. There is some 'race to the treasure' aspect, as the 1st there gets 1st pick, but all who contributed to the rules get slices, except when there's a curse.

There is as much 'race to the amulet' pressure, as these both protect from curses, and allow extra actions.

It makes a nice light game, with a lot of social interaction as the treasure locations are constrained, but it's not terribly deep. The bits are boffo- some of the best components I've ever seen- but the game play isn't too challenging.

I'll throw out a 6.5 on a 1st solo 4-player game, nice, and nice to look at, but not going to be a special favorite of mine I expect.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Jó játék, én szeretem és többször játszottam játékmesterként rendezvényeken és azoknak akiknek megmutattam szintén tetszett.  Ha az ember megérti a kincskeresés és a nyomkártyák lerakásának mechanizmusát, egy könnyed szórakoztató játék. 


A játékban 2-4 játékos kincsekre vadászik egy szigeten. A sziget három lapból állítható össze. A lapok mindkét oldalán térképrészlet van (ABC és abc) jelölésekkel. Ha kiszámoljuk ez összesen 16 variációt tesz lehetővé és miután a táblák összeillesztésének kétfajta lehetősége van – attól függően, hogy feljebb vagy lejjebb toljuk őket – így a térkép összesen 32 módon rakható össze. Emellett az újrajátszhatóságot biztosítja az is, hogy a térképen lévő hatszögekre véletlenszerűen helyezzük fel a három pálmafát, a négy házikót és három kőszobrot (amik a  Húsvét-szigetekien lévő moai szobrokra emlékeztetnek). 
A játék egyik érdekes mechanizmusa ahogy a kincsekre vadászunk. Ugyanis a kincseknek nincsenek meghatározott helyei, hanem mi maguk határozzuk meg a négyfajta (szürke, fehér, fekete, barna) kincs helyét. A szigeten található tengerpartok, bozótosok, tavak, folyók, helyek és dzsungelek. Illetve ezeken található az általunk tetszőlegesen felrakott pálmafák, kőszobrok és házikók. Hatfajta nyomkártya segítségével határozzuk meg a kincsek helyét. Ezek  meghatározzák, 
-	hogy a kincs valamely területen található (pld. tengerparton, legnagyobb dzsungelben stb.) 
-	vagy éppen nem ott található (pld. nincs tavon, folyón stb.)
-	vagy a kincs valami közvetlen közelében van (pld. folyó mellett, szobor mellett)
-	vagy egy vagy két távolságra tőle (pld. tengerparttól egy vagy kettő távolságra, pálmafától egy vagy két távolságra)
-	vagy nem a terepfajta mellett van (pld. nincs közvetlenül dzsungel mellett)
-	vagy pedig látótávolságon kívül van (pld. a kunyhótól több mint két távolságra van)
Minden nyomkártya csökkenti a lehetséges helyek számát és nem lehet ellentétes a korábbi nyomokkal. Minden nyomkártyát az adott kincs alá rakjuk és rátesszük a jelölőnket, jelezve, hogy ezt az információt mi adtuk a kincshez. Így szép sorban csökkentjük a lehetséges helyek számát, sorba vesszük le a térképről a jelölőket, míg már csak egyetlen jelölő marad. Ezután a szigeten lévő autónkkal el lehet kocsikázni odáig és aki felteszi a kincset szintén egy jelölőt rakhat a kincs alá. 
A játék másik érdekes mechanizmusa a kincskártyák szétosztása. A kincskártyákon 2-től 6-ig aranyérmék (gyakorlatban győzelmi pontok) találhatók, illetve két átokkártya is van. Annyi kincset veszünk a pakliból, mint amennyi jelölő a kincs alatt van, ebből mindenki annyit kap a kezébe, amennyi jelölőt az adott kincshez letett. Mindenki megnézi majd ezeket összekeverjük (hozzákeverve egy plusz kártyát is). Majd sorba csapjuk fel a kincskártyákat és alulról felfelé haladva sorba eldöntik a játékosok, hogy kérik-e a kincset (tehát aki  megtalálta a kincset választ először) vagy pedig vár egy jobb kincskártyára. Aki elviszi a kártyát, elveszi a jelölőjét és jön a következő kártya. Ha átokkártyát húzunk fel, akkor megszakad a kincs osztás és akinek van még jelölője a kincs alatt annak vissza kell adni a legértékesebb kincsét, hacsak nem tud amulettet beadni helyette. 

A kincskeresés és a kincs szétosztása a játék lényege, bár a leírások szerint az igazi taktikát az amulettek jelentik a játékban. Ugyanis a kincsek kiosztása után a kőszobrok 60 fokot elfordulnak és ahová a tengerpartra néznek a víz egy amulettet sodor a partra. Ezeket pedig az autóval való mozgás közben veheti fel az ember. Az amuletteket többféle módon felhasználhatjuk:
-	védelem az átokkártya ellen
-	extra akcióként két nyomkártyát rakhatunk le, vagy még egyszer mozgathatjuk autónkat
-	vagy le lehet venni egy kincsjelölőt a térképről ezzel csökkentve a lehetséges kincslelőhelyek számát
-	kezünkben lévő nyomkártyákat kicserélhetjük és rögtön használhatjuk az új kártyákat.
A kártyacsere egyébként amulett nélkül is lehetséges csak akkor mást nem csinálhatunk. Ezt a szabályt érdekes módon csak az amuletteknél írják le, ez mindenképpen a szabálykönyv hibája. 

Mindent egybe véve egy remek családi játék, amely szerintem már két fővel is jól működik, bár 3-4 fővel még jobb. Gyönyörű kivitel és jól működő mechanizmusok jellemzik. Kezdőknek is bátran ajánlható. A Piatniknak köszönhetően sok helyen kapható, ráadásul nem megfizethetetlen áron
http://jemmagazin.hu/tobago/

RATING: 7
The board looks nothing like Tobago.  At first glance this looks like a deduction game but actually it is a game of majorities.  The number of shares of the treasure you get is determined by the number of clue cards you play.  A card can only be played if it restricts the number of possible locations, while leaving at least one valid location.

RATING: 6

You play clue cards to localise a treasure that may be nowhere near your jeep, while finding the treasure itself offers no more privilege than first refusal on the spoils. RATING: 7
Tobago is a fun game, strong in theme, and first rate production value.  The game board is beautifully illustrated with vivid colors depicting a tropical island.  The game also includes small jeeps and palm trees and huts that are seldom, if ever, surpassed in quality.  Players play clue cards to rule out possible locations of four different buried treasures that they are competing to find.  The game board is made of 3 parts that are printed on both sides and can be interlocked in different ways, enabling the game board to be configured several different ways.  I am a huge fan of game boards that cane be reconfigured because each game feels like a different experience.  Overall, I enjoy this game.  It is not a deep game, so I wouldn't want to play it over and over again.

RATING: 5.8
Feb10:

Part of the game is awesome. Then they decided "Let's take this logical family game, and put in the quirkiest, most awkward treasure distribution ever!"

The system overly rewards the person who contributed the most to a treasure. Instead of just getting a larger share of the treasure, they get a larger share of the BETTER treasure in most cases. Not only that, but the actual decisions during the treasure distribution aren't even that interesting.

If I play again? I'm just saying deal out 1 treasure per token; none of the rest of that crap. Leave the Curses out. It would cut the game time significantly and get down to the meat that was interesting.

It could win the Sdj, and it is pretty and entertaining, but it isn't a Keltis that's for sure.

RATING: 9
A very strong game in which you are attempting to locate treasure on an island map by slowly whittling down the possible locations for it, before racing to collect it and secure the booty.

I absolutely love the mechanics in this game, each card you play must reduce the possible locations of a piece of treasure and every time you contribute you effectively get an extra share of that treasure once it's found.  You also have to manage the location of your piece on the map, and attempt to periodically collect other artifacts to help your expedition.  This creates a nice balance between the cards and the movement on the board throughout the game.  So-called "curse" cards ensure that only players who balance these aspects properly are likely to receive good treasure and ultimately win the game.

In my opinion, a great game for families and strategy gamers alike.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Players play cards that narrow the possible location of 1 of 4 treasures. The object is to narrow the location of treasures until there is only one possibility. Players who contributed to defining the location of a treasure take part in its bounty. When the treasure deck runs out, the game is over.

This is an attractive family game but with no real game arc and only a mild level of control. The treasure deck could be 40, 60 or 80 cards and the only thing that would change is game length - the game has no real beginning, middle or end. It seems best to spread your influence to all treasures and take good advantage of the amulets, which give strong advantages if you can get them. It's different and novel enough from other family games that it's a keeper for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've only played once and I really like Tobago, but both myself and the person I played with found it a bit frustrating because one has to learn all the symbols and what this could mean (in sight of X, next to X, largest X, Not in largest X). When you get a treasure map going, the various symbols can get a bit confusing, so we had to keep referring to the rules which slowed play down. Too, analysis paralysis set in a few times. Still, I think Tobago is a beautifully designed game, and I'd like to see how it plays with more players, so my rating might still go up.

RATING: 7
A very elegantly designed game fit for the family. The quality of everything is outstanding. When playing, must remember to add the amulets after each treasure! 

IMPORTANT: There is a 'missing' option C) in addition to A) Playing a clue card and B) Moving your ATV. Namely, you may discard your entire hand and draw a new set of map cards. This rule is only mentioned in the Amulet section at the very end.

There are some deduction elements, but I felt the game was more about anticipation, planning and some good old fashioned cost-benefit analysis. This is more of an abstract bidding/auction game.

Weight : Tikal > Tobago > Incan Gold

RATING: 7
Despite an irresistible theme and fresh mechanics, I have to raise an eyebrow at Tobago. Ostensibly you're hunting for treasure on island with rivers, plains mountains, beaches, forests, and ancient statue gods. The game throws up the illusion of deduction by having players narrow down the possible locations of each treasure, but the locations aren't fixed and you're not using any clues to actually deduce anything. Instead, the players just decide, step by step, where the treasures will materialize. On my turn I could say that it's in the mountains, near the beach, close to an idol, etc. It's up to me, so long as I don't contradict a previous player's decision. So, like Mr. Jack, Tobago turns out to be a hex game about reading and manipulating space, without any deduction at all. Fair enough.

Strangely, though, I'm not convinced the map matters. Each card you play will later give you the chance to collect some treasure, and you can play a card every single turn. All scoring, all the time. It doesn't matter which player spends a turn picking up a treasure instead of playing a card, because the rewards are all but identical. You can press your luck, position for slightly better opportunities, and claim bonus actions, but when it comes down to it, the deduction is fake and the map is largely irrelevant. It's a short, light game with an appealing theme, some remarkable components, and constant scoring for everyone. Perhaps the recipe for a family game?

Fairly unique. Plays pretty good with 2 players and my wife really likes it. Very easy to explain and beautiful components.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a very clever game!

The map (modular with 16 board configurations) has different terrain types and statues, palm trees and huts are placed pseudo-randomly on the board. There are 4 types of treasures depicted by different colour cubes.  

Players attempt to find the treasures by placing clue cards from their hands into the column of known clues for a given treasure. Clues must provide information to help find the treasure, for example a clue might say that the treasure is in the largest mountain region or that it's within one space of a hut. Players place one of their markers on the clue cards they play out.

On a turn a player either plays a clue card or moves their truck around the board. Often the truck is moved to pick up a treasure that has been narrowed down to a single space. The person then adds one of their player markers to the bottom of the clue column for that treasure, then the treasure is allocated. 

For each marker a player has in the clue column (either from playing clue cards or finding the treasure with their truck) they are dealt a treasure card. Treasure cards range from 2 to 6 in value. All treasures are then collected together and shuffled with one additional unseen treasure card. A random treasure is revealed and starting from the bottom of the clue column the player whose marker is present can decide to take the treasure or pass, if everyone passes the treasure is discarded. If someone takes the treasure their marker is removed from the column and the next treasure is flipped up and offered first to the person whose marker is lowest in the column.

Whoever takes the final treasure gets to place a new clue card into the now empty clue column for this treasure, and they place a player marker on the card.

The game ends when the treasure card deck is exhausted. 

This kind-of deduction mechanic and the treasure card allocation are brilliant, there are some fun things to think about especially when trying to decide which clue card to play into which column. The game is not exceptionally deep, there are a few interesting things to worry about but nothing that causes preoccupation. That being said I find the game very fun to play and the theme really comes through and helps make the experience enjoyable.

[b]Rating Scale[/b]
1.0 components / design
1.0 mechanics 
1.0 Learnable / Teachable
1.0 re-playability / Frequency

Total Score 9.5

[b]Thoughts[/b]
Great easy game.  It is fun to play with new players and seasoned players.  It has unique mechanics for treasure finding.  You place clues, collectively, to narrow down where the possibility of a treasure is.  Once you narrow it to one specific place, each player who contributed clues gets to be in the dig.  The more clues, the more treasure you can come home with.

It is so different from other games, it really stands on its own.  I play it several times a year.  It is easy for friends to learn an enjoy.

RATING: 7
This is one beautifully produced game!  I really enjoyed the whole aspect of clue giving in developing the treasure map for each of the four treasures in the game on the island and how each time you contribute a card ensures you a chance to pick up some treasure - placement of where you are does matter to some degree as well! Shades of a refined and beautified Old Town exist.

RATING: 8
Don't be fooled into thinking this is deduction; it's not. There are 4 piles to play into. The clue cards are rules. Once a few rules are in place you can place the cubes from a particular pile out on the board adhering to the rules as a visual aid. 

Your real goal is to steer the last cube/cubes toward your area to you can score the color and get first choice on treasures.

There are some real decisions to be made here, though the luck element still leaves a good deal of guess work. This should be played at a brisk pace for best results.

It currently sits on the bubble to see how it fares after repeated plays.

RATING: 6
You are exploring an island in an SUV to find treasure.  You get pieces of a map that reduces the number of hexes where the treasure can be.  When there is one location remaining, you claim the treasure by moving to the space where it is.  

The game is very investing in that it teaches you basic set theory.  This is a valuable skill for computer programming and database design.  

The game feels a bit samey over time, but it is one of daughters favorite games.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tobago is a Clue deduction game, each player can place a clue (add a new piece to the treasure map) that gives an indication to the treasures whereabouts.
Once the treasures location has been confirmed via positive and negative logic (eg. it's not on a beach), then the players will race to uncover the treasure.
The treasures size is indicated by the amount of clue pieces that were used to find it.
The treasure is then divided amongst the players who contributed to finding the treasure via clues or the actual uncovering, the more clues you add the bigger your share, the later on in the piece you contribute the better your treasure choices.

Across The Board
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If you are looking for a slightly advanced family game with lots of fun, clever reverse deduction, great pieces, pirates and cursed treasure stop reading this and go out and get Tobago!

This is a family favourite. The board, pieces and presentation of the game is fantastic. The island board can be set up in 32 different ways and there is always a largest and smallest of each terrain type whatever the set up. Did I say this game was clever? Up to 4 players are treasure hunters scooting all over the map in jeeps (landrovers for the Brits) and collectively via a brilliant reverse deduction mechanic adding to treasure maps that whittle down the possible locations of 4 different coloured treasures (we say the treasures of pirates Brownbeard, Whitebeard, Greybeard and Blackbeard). 

On your go you either play a map card to one of the four treasures (belonging to previously mentioned hirsute pirates) thereby reducing the possibilities for its proverbial X on the map, or, you move your jeep (landrover really, as everyone knows they are the best rough terrain vehicle) across the map trying to jocky yourself to be in the best position to get there first once the final resting place of the pirates pension fund is discovered.

If this wasn't enough fantastic-ness in a box the process of sharing out the dug up treasures is brilliant. Everyone gets a treasure card (values 1 - 6) for each part of the map they contributed to finding the treasure, you also get a treasure card if you dug up the treasure. You look at these values and then hand them back to the "dealer" who adds another treasure card blind and shuffles them up. So the more you contributed to finding the treasure, the more info you have of its value and the more of it you are likely to get. But don't forget there are two curses in the deck. So it maybe that the curse was the extra unseen card that was shuffled in ... or it may be that one of your fellow treasure hunters now know the treasure is cursed, or you may be the only one who knows and are now looking to bail out and grab anything of value as soon as you can. 

The treasure is distributed by offering it one at a time to the players who helped find it starting with the player who dug it up, and then back up the map piece line. So you may know there are a couple of six's in there so you may refuse the first few 2's or 3's as you are waiting for the 6's ... but also if someone starts taking anything of low value you get the idea the treasure is cursed and they are grabbing what they can as as soon as a curse comes out you are all doomed forever! Well not quite you lose your highest value treasure you have already collected throughout the game and the rest of the current treasure is not distributed ... that is unless you have picked up a magic amulet. 

Did I mention the game has magic amulets, that mysteriously seem to be found along the coastline after the majestical turning of the statues. These magic amulets let you do .. well they let you do almost anything ... well loads of stuff at least. Too much for my weary fingers to relate here.

It was fantastic watching my 7yr old son in our last game. He pulled a face when looking at his treasure card but then it quickly evaporated and he remained silent, not his usual self. We all cottoned on to the fact that he has difficulty coping with the stress of certain situations in gaming. He had just seen the cursed treasure. We all started hoovering up the low treasures that were coming out, anything would do, and as he was last to get a choice (he laid down the first 2 map pieces) no treasure was reaching him for him to collect. We were all out of chances to get the treasure and sat back satisfied we had baled before the curse came out. The next card was a six, he collected it, then a five, he took it again with a smile ..."fooled you" he laughed, there was no curse ... we had just been truly played. Oscar for Jack!

This is a great family game, did I mention it was really clever?

RATING: 8
Fantastic gateway game! It's definitely on the light side, which only makes me want to get it to the table more often. The components (and insert) are outstanding, by the way!

Update 4/26/15: After a second play, I wanted to expand on my former comments by listing some pros and cons for Tobago. 

Pros: 
+Great components, art and insert (some of the best I've seen)
+Unique. I don't have anything else like it in my collection (reverse deduction)
+It's interactive and fun. Even in a 2 player game, you are constantly watching and playing off of your opponents moves. The distribution of treasure adds to the interactivity in a positive way. 

Cons:

-It's swingy. The game can create considerable swings in a single turn. Depending on how the treasure comes out (mostly luck of the draw) you can go immediately from first to last, or the other way around. 
-It runs a little long for what it is. We've played it twice now and both times were 90 minutes or more. If it played in 45 minutes to an hour (max), the game would be more appealing. 

Overall, a solid and unique game with what seems like great legs.

RATING: 8
Ordered November 16, 2009

Played multiple times during Great Lakes Games 2009 and had a blast.  It's light, easy to teach, and fun. Player interaction is moderate without a lot of opportunity to screw your neighbor. Some of the card iconography takes a bit getting used to. It's common to not have enough cubes to indicate where a treasure lives early in its life depending on what card starts the row (e.g. Not on the beach) so get used to keeping track of where treasures are even when there are no cubes on the board to indicate it. Takes some mental gyrations and some spatial skills to see how your cards affect (reduce) the likely locations of the treasures.

Traded in BGG Math Trade - Dec 2013

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a great family game.  It is nice and simple to pick up and it is still enjoyable even with a fair amount of luck involved.

In Tobago the players are treasure hunters on an island.  At all times there are 4 different treasures that players are trying to locate on the island.  This is done by playing clue cards that eliminate possible locations of a treasure.  For example, a card may say that the treasure is on a beach, later somebody could add that it is also next to a river, then somebody could say it isn't within two spaces of a hut.  Eventually the location of the treasure is down to only one possible spot.  Then one of the players needs to drive their atv over to the spot and dig it up.  Your reward for digging up the treasure and for adding a clue card is a stake in the treasure.  The treasure cards range from 2 to 6 coins.  In reverse order that you place clues people are offered treasure cards in which they can pass, letting the next person choose, or they can take it.

This sounds nice, but of course there needs to be some twists as well.  After a few treasures have been dug up the treasure cards have a couple of curses randomly mixed in.  When these come up everybody who had a stake in the treasure lose out.  Also, you need an amulet (these are spread around the island and can be picked up with your atv) to protect yourself or you lose the best treasure card you've received so far.  After all the treasure cards have been depleted the game ends and players count up their coins.

I think this works great for a family, or some new gamers.  For sure this is a great gate-way game.

[b]Game Length:[/b] (Short) :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar: (Long)
[b]Complexity:[/b] (Easy) :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: (Complex)
[b]Accessibility:[/b] (Non-Gamers) :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: (Gamers)
[b]Luck Factor:[/b] (Luck Based) :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: (Strategy Based)
[b]Re-playability:[/b] (Low) :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar: (High)
[b]Value for Time:[/b] (Waste) :star::star::halfstar::nostar::nostar: (Worth)

RATING: 6.5
I like to think of this as a reverse deduction game. Thematically, I'm discovering clues that narrow down where the treasure is hidden. But in reality, I am choosing which clues to play on treasures of choice, deciding where the treasure should be. This is a delightful switch, as I can push it closer to me for easy pickup, or stack the deck in my favor, or spread myself thin to get a little piece of every pie. A fun, fast-playing game that offers quite a bit of flavor as well.

RATING: 8
Competitive island treasure hunting game, only you decide where the treasure is! This simple but ingenious idea makes this an interestingly cerebral experience, in which you carefully narrow down the location of the treasure whilst manoeuvring about the island and positioning yourself to get to it when they are finally pin pointed.

The key to winning seems to be getting your stake in all chest locations as much as possible, with actually getting the chests themselves only slightly more valuable.

Modular board, simple play and 40 minutes or less game play makes this a very satisfying experience every time!

RATING: 6
Treasure hunting is a play I enjoyed in childhood and it's very well implemented in Tobago. Play clue cards to reduce the number of available spots ("in the jungle", "next to a statue" etc.) or ensure to dig up the treasure yourself to participate in the sharing of the loot. The sharing mechanism is also interesting as you can accept a share or decline, hoping that the next share will be better (but it may be cursed, causing you to lose a share).

The gameplay is pretty much the same throughout and between games so I don't see much depth but for a family game, Tobago would be my first choice.

RATING: 6
Relatively simple game with poorly written and organized rules.  It's a deduction game with some "wild cards" (emeralds) that can be used to do some fiddly things (a free move, discarded hand, remove a potential treasure location, etc).  Personally, I find the game lacking in tension.  There is a puzzle-ish sense to narrowing down where a treasure can be.  And each player wants to be in on the "decision tree" for each treasure.  But the game is somewhat sterile.  I got in after watching Rhado's Runthrough, and he made is look like a blast... It's not.  Not a bad game, mind you, but just no drama to it.  There is never really a sense of the big score.  There is no real sense of struggle.  It's more like a game of Blokus (which I don't particularly care for).  All that said, I would highly recommend this an "educational game" to be played with 10 - 15 year-olds.  Also has nice components.

RATING: 5
There are four treasures at any point in time buried on Tobago.  Players play cards to specific treasure to pinpoint where on Tobago each treasure is located.  Each card further limits the spots that the treasure can be, black treasure is in the biggest forest, two spaces away from a hut, and next to a palm tree.

After discovering where the treasure is people than travel to 'dig' up the treasure. After the treasure is found everyone that was a part of pinpointing that treasure (played a card to the treasure) has a chance at some points...or the dreaded curse card.  

It's a good family game that may start to drag towards the end.

RATING: 6
[b]Tobago[/b] was traded away in a math trade 3/12/14. (Received Jamaica)

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]As a family we never really got into Tobago. The bits were beautiful, but there were parts of the game that really fell flat for us. Our two biggest problems were the SUV movement and the treasure mechanic. As far as the SUV movement was concerned it was not very intuitive and if you started using amulets you really had too pay attention to how many movement actions you used. The push your luck element with the treasure deck was never that exciting; sometimes it felt like you did all this work only to get nothing because a curse popped up before you had a chance to grab some loot.

In the end we loved playing cards to locate the treasure, but that was not enough to save it from being traded after a few plays.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- Component quality: This game has some beautiful wooden huts, palm trees and jeeps plus these amazing Easter Island statues. 
The board is made up of 3 sections that can be attached in different ways to create over 30 different maps.
The game oozes theme thanks to the beautiful components.
- We love narrowing down where treasure is located by playing cards, a really innovative mechanic.
- Amulets: add some variety to the game by letting you perform extra actions and protecting you from curses.
- Driving your jeep around the board is just fun.
- I love the push your luck element to disperse the treasure. 

I believe this is an excellent family/gateway game with easy to pick up rules. It is not a game we will play every day/week, but I can see us pulling it out once or twice a month and enjoying it.[/i]

RATING: 5
100517
Played 2 player with 6 year old. Didn't like the random setup rules of the huts, palm trees and huts. Felt it was a very lazy design decision. Didn't like the fact that the optimal starting position is always at where an amulet will be appearing. Didn't like the possibility of using several amulets in a turn to chain a series of events and potentially completing and collecting a treasure card by yourself. Felt like I didn't have many decisions to make and deciding which clue card to place at which treasure was often a random affair.

Enjoyed the unique way of distributing treasures the first playthrough with my wife but found the use of the amulets added a little too much complexity to the game to make it a truly great gateway game. Need to play with more players to see how it scales.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
You're either placing a card to eliminate possible treasure spaces (and by doing so placing yourself in the treasure dissemination queue) or moving your car around to a found treasure location to get the treasure distributed (and get the best bit for yourself). Turns aren't as fast as I'd hope for in a perceived family game, and it's not as kid friendly as I expected as there's quite a lot of choice for your card play (4 cards across 4 different locations). The amulets then make things more complicated by allowing for clever moves. Which is all good for the gaming group though, where it's a decent game with great bits, and which stands out a bit because it's different from the predominant genres out there. But ... there's huge, huge luck in the treasure cards that come out. A treasure distribution may net 1 player 12 points and another 4 points for exactly the same effort (turns expended). Another player may get nothing (as the pus card skull and crossbones) comes out and see their efforts wasted. So the main strategy is just to play your cards so that treasures get narrowed down as fast as possible to a location right near a car on the board. After all no one's going to want to spend multiple turns driving across the board to discover treasure when they could be concentrating on treasure closer to them. If everybody's playing this similarly, well then it comes down to luck - having the right narrowing down cards, getting good treasure cards ... so it's not much a gamer's game either. Nice components though
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This game is deduction done backwards: instead of narrowing down the possibilities to find out where the treasure is, you're narrowing them down to determine where the treasure is.  The idea is to play a card that eliminates all the possibilities except for the one right where your car happens to be, or at least close.  This is a very innovative idea, and while you're still subject to the luck of the card draw, the game is pretty engaging.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Tobago has a cute mechanism.  The players are trying to find treasure on and island.  To do so they play clue cards.  These cards convey information like "the treasure is in the biggest lake" or "the treasure is next to a tree".

The players apply these cards to one of the several colors of treasure, i.e. the white treasure might be in the biggest lake and next to a tree.  Eventually the process of elimination will reduce the number of potential treasure locations to an individual space.  At which point the first player to that spot "finds" the treasure.

Every clue a player places on a treasure gives that player a part of the reward when that treasure is found.  So it's kind of like players are investing in stocks.

There's a few other minor mechanisms to keep the game moving and add some structure.  It's fun to puzzle out the best way to apply clues so that it benefits you the most.  Players share treasure payouts so there's a bit of maneuvering to make sure you net the largest payout.

A simple family game based on a cool idea.  However, I have plenty simple family games already so I'd never buy it for myself.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I'm really glad that I own Tobago because it is very different than anything else I own.  Many people have likened Tobago to clue in reverse and I guess I can see where those people are coming from.  It's kind of a deduction game in reverse.  It seems similar to Android in that each player defines where the treasure is located just like in Android each player defines the level of guiltiness of the suspects. (Note: I have not played Android, so I am making a comparison based on what I've read about Android.)

I spent a surprisingly large amount of the game laughing in delight of placing clues.  "It's within two spaces of a hut.  Then, it's not next to a hut."  Ha ha ha!  Delightful.  

Is Tobago a brain burner?  No.  Is it deep?  No.  Is it worth owning and playing?  Certainly.  It's a delightful and not very stressful game.  It's a palate cleanser amd a game that I'm very pleased to own.
