RATING: 6
played: 1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
By all means, I should not like this game. The theme doesn't really make sense. I like to pretend the players are all amnesiac pirates who can't remember where they hid their own treasure, so they are driving around the island trying to jog their memory by looking at familiar landmarks. After you spend a number of turns writing the treasure map, someone will swoop in and dig up the treasure and everyone who helped write the map will get to take a quick peek before blindly sticking their hand in the treasure chest, creating a chaotic push your luck element that usually ends up with some lucky chump getting a six gold card they didn't even know existed.

So, why do I like Tobago? Well, even though the theme falls apart under scrutiny, I really do feel like a treasure hunter, and those wild swings of luck usually end with players erupting in joyous hooting and hollering or playful shaking of fists. In fact, the inly real complaint I have about the game is the lack of cubes and how placing them in potential treasure spots can sometimes be rather tedious. Overall, don't take Tobago seriously. It's fast and silly, and surprisingly good despite its flaws.

RATING: 7
[b]Caveat:[/b] I’ve only played this as a 2-player game, my :thumbsup: and :thumbsdown: are based on that.

:thumbsup: An original theme.
:thumbsup: Very nice components, with clear iconography. Completely language independent. 
:thumbsup: Easy rules with interesting choices. You really want to make sure you have a (small) stake in every treasure, while getting the timing of when to pick your share just right, and don't let the other players do all the digging.

----

:thumbsdown: I think the 'quality' of gameplay depends on the number of players. With two, it's relatively easy to stay on each others heels while chasing treasures, which makes it more difficult to best one another with clever card play. I'll have to see if this gets better with higher player counts. 
:thumbsdown: This is just a nitpick, but I would've preferred pirate meeples instead of the ATV's. But that's nothing I can't remedy...

RATING: 6
A little different from other games I play, this beautiful looking game is simple and offers enough decisions on my turn to keep me entertained. Not to be confused with a deduction game - the game is really about elimination. You're targeting treasure based on your cards available, where your car is positioned and the investment already made in that treasure. It's all done in a simple way and while it doesn't set the house on fire I find it rather relaxing. No stress, no angst, just simple choices and a bit of fun. Unfortunately I find some of the language used in the English rules around amulets and raising treasure at the start of your movement to be a little confusing. Rules discussion and scratching of heads around the table do slow down the game somewhat. Kind of reminds me of Mesopotamia, which isn't necessarily a good thing. But not quite as creeping step, step, step as Mesopotamia due to the amulets.

RATING: 3
A rare original family game of the classic type. The core mechanic is innovative, the theme and visuals are a bit different (or at least rare), I'm just not entirely convinced at how it all comes together. 

The anti-deduction (narrowing the space where a treasure is) is interesting, but takes a while for new players to get. The thing that bothers me most is the small increment in money - I see the game is made to be balanced, but it's also pretty unexciting. +2 coins, +4 coins and so on. I wish the game's basic premise could somehow be streamlined even more, or they found some way to instil more tension if the game. As it is, it's a charming game, I really enjoy the type of deduction the game asks of its players, but lacks a certain excitement and engagement. I have the feeling there are just too many mechanisms and pieces in the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
As far as family-friendly games go, Tobago is pretty ace. It combines an original treasure-hunting mechanism with an equally original treasure-dividing auction, where every bit you contribute to locating the treasure earns you a portion of the loot. Despite appearing simple, it generates a fair bit of angst as players push their luck in getting better loot when dividing the treasure. Adding to the theme are the extremely attractive components, especially the tiny stone heads.  

There are a couple of caveats, however. The 'family-friendly' term is rather subjective, and my personal experience is mixed – the gameplay can be a little difficult to explain due to the less-than-optimal layout of the rule book. Also, players have had a tough time visualising the remaining sites where treasure can be found especially if the map is heavy with clues which state where the treasure [i]can't[/i] be found. The last snag is that amulets are a bit too powerful as a catch-all item and it's easy to forget the variety of abilities they provide. As a result, players may attempt to hoard them to blow them all in one big turn, which generally isn't much fun for other players.

But really, I personally love this game quite a bit. It has a few cutthroat moments where players swipe treasure before other players can reach it, as well as those big surprises when a curse turns up unexpectedly. All this means I'm quite willing to overlook some of the duller moments in the game (which mostly involves grabbing the amulets).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Players are treasure hunters: they place clue cards against treasure hoards and try to narrow down the location of said treasure. They then have to drive their 4WD to pick up treasure. Most treasures wins!

Ok, I'll admit it: the logic element in setting up the location of the treasure is clever and unique.

But that's about it.

The rest of the game is standard procedural Euro fare. Action points, setting yourself up to score big etc.

The Curse cards are too flukey IMHO. Ditch them when you play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Okay, I have to say, I am a budding game designer. As such, I sometimes notice that certain game designs remind me of games I might have created myself. There have even been several occasions where I've  "invented"  a mechanic or theme that, low and behold, a new game comes out utilizing. "Damn, they beat me to it" i say. Tobago though - holy crap - how the heck did Bruce Allen come up with this? I would never be clever enough to create a mechanic like this one. 

In Tobago players search for treasure by getting cards that depict where the treasure is or is not, but only relative to other objects on the board - and these objects change position each game, and the board is variable as well. So a card may depict something like "the treasure in not within two spaces of a hut. Then another card may illustrate that the treasure is also next to a forest. As more cards are exposed, the location of the booty gets narrowed down until it can only possibly be in one spot - then its a race to get it and unearth it. Really a brilliant, novel design for a game - makes me wonder if there was a time after countless playtests and reworkings that Mr Allen said "oh my gosh, this actually works!" 

Another great thing about the game are the excellent components - some of the best i've seen in any game - and the board is very beautiful as well. They call this a "family" game, but I think there is more strategy then one may think. Lots of luck involved, but this is totally permissible for a game in this weight class. Really though, even if I didn't like playing this game (which I do indeed) I would still have to give it up for the brilliant design.

RATING: 7
It's a deduction-themed game without any actual deduction! Four treasures will be available at any given time. On your turn, you may play a card that narrows the location of one of these (e.g., in the largest lake, 1 or 2 spaces from a hut, etc.) or move your ATV (to pick up amulets, which give you extra turns or help avoid curses, or to dig up the treasure -- once it's location has been narrowed down to 1 hex). When a treasure is found, each player who contributed to the search (by playing cards, or by driving their ATV to the final location) gets to look at 1 treasure card (valued 2-6, with 2 curses mixed into the bottom of the deck) per contribution. An additional card is shuffled into those, then players (in reverse order of contribution) get to go through these cards one by one, choosing either to take the revealed card or pass. If a curse turns up, the hunt ends immediately and all contributors still waiting for cards must discard their most valuable treasure, or 1 amulet. The game ends after the treasure deck is depleted; most treasure wins!

The board and bits are very nice, and the game plays very smoothly: thematic, fun ... I can see why so many think this is a good SdJ nominee. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is quite an original game that I knew nothing about going in.  It all started with me being handed a palm tree, stone head, and hut and being told to plop them down anywhere on the map.  What followed was a sort of build-you-own treasure map experience.  The game is played by playing clue tokens on one of 4 treasures to narrow down the treasure's location and moving around a map to collect those treasures.  There are a couple other elements like cursed treasures and magical medallions but that's basically the entire game.

As you can tell from that description the game is simple and easy to teach.  It also played reasonably quickly.  With setup and explanation a game with 3 people took only 75 minutes.  While I've played games with deductive reasoning elements before the theme and way this game handled it made it feel very new and I had a lot of fun.  Despite the simplicity there are also a lot of interesting choices to make just under the surface.

It also deserves mentioning that the production values of this game are phenomenal.  The pieces are all solid, beautiful and well-made and it comes with a modular board with an advertised 32 different configurations.  And all without a Splotter price tag.

It's not a game I'd want to play all the time (which is the only thing keeping me from throwing it on my wish list right away) but it is a fun and engaging experience with very fresh feeling gameplay.

RATING: 7
[Zoch 2009 edition] {Adventure, Gateway; hand-management, bluffing, racing; scalable}  Tobago is an attractive and thematic gateway game.  Player interaction is strong!  The rules are a little difficult to learn but players can manage them soon after the game starts.  It is a very good gateway game.  It is fun, esp. when players succeeded in raising a treasure. It is very luck and is only a tactical game.  And, players are at the hand of other players in the treasure hunting; a player can easily destroy the game plan of the opponents with clue cards.  The decisions in getting treasure cards are more meaningful than I expected - being the last player to collect a treasure card is more rewarding; thus, it attracts player to take risk to stay to the last.  Getting amulet is also more important, both for more tactical options or defending.  The score in a typical game is tight, losing a 5- or 6-treasure card is very substantial; thus, one need an amulet to safeguard the treasure cards.  In addition, one has to balance the movement of ATV and playing clue cards.  Playing more clue cards allows one to have more treasure cards but it also reduces one's initiative in raising a treasure.  Finally, the starting placement of ATV is important; don't blindly place the ATV on the board.  Place it wisely! So this game is not totally luck. Decisions are more meaningful than I expected.

The components are awesome! I love the ATV, trees and statues.  The illustration is colorful and attractive.  The board setup is clever.  I love this game!

RATING: 9
L‘avventura può iniziare!
Le casse ricolme d’oro di un favoloso tesoro sono sepolte sull’isola. Una cassa dovrebbe essere a fianco ad una capanna, vicino alla più grande montagna dell’isola: lo dicono i frammenti della mappa del tesoro che possiedi. Un’altra cassa deve essere là dove si sente forte il rumore di un fiume selvaggio. Infine, un’altra sotto i rami curvi di una fiera palma in prossimità di una statua che emette rumori sinistri …mentre sembra ruotare…

Benvenuti su Tobago!

Lasciati sedurre dai paesaggi delle illustrazioni, dal fascino particolare dell’ambientazione e dalle dettagliate pedine. Decodifica i frammenti della mappa del tesoro insieme agli altri avventurieri che sono sbarcati sull’isola. Avverti crescere la tensione. Chi raggiungerà per primo il luogo in cui giace una cassa piena d’oro? Con le tre parti della mappa da gioco potrete creare trentadue isole differenti che vi assicureranno partite sempre diverse e sfide emozionanti.

Elenco elementi di gioco:
1 Libretto delle regole
1 Foglio supplementare
3 Sezioni di mappa (doppia faccia)
39 Carte Tesoro
2 Carte Maledizione
52 Carte Frammento
68 Segnalini Posizione
3 Fermi
4 Fuoristrada
4 Capanne
3 Palme
3 Statue
20 Amuleti
60 Sigilli

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
http://www.boardgamegeek.com/listitem/3161909?commentid=4561006#comment4561006

"try the Clicker-Spiele games. They all use the same mechanic as Tobago but are much, much better"

Schinderhannes
Old Town
Café Melange

http://www.boardgamegeek.com/listitem/3161909?commentid=4561180#comment4561180

"In general all three of the above are deeper games that Tobago. In Tobago, I found it was an unclear advantage to remove more or less cubes when you place a card. Which meant that most of the depth was simply in the board movement, as well as pushing your luck when you draw treasures.

In the Clicker games, it is absolutely critical that you maximize the cards by playing them at moments that they give you the most information. Now you could argue that adds luck, but it really doesn't because you have 3-6 piles (depending on the game) of cards to chose from and that's a big part of the strategy. You can also play multiple cards a turn, creating these awesome chain reactions if you plan for them correctly.

Cafe Melange, also has an added layer to this where you are trying to minimize the final location of specific pieces into specific spots, to get the most victory points.

FWIW, If you ask me, Schinderhannes is best with 2, Cafe Melange is best with 3. Old Town is a little behind those two but has the best theme."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.3
tl;dr: good family deduction game

I quite like deduction games. This one lets you influence the outcome though with cards you play. I quite like it, and it has nice artwork and components. Where I get annoyed with this game is that you can have a great plan and play great, but still get screwed by luck of the draw. If you don't get the cards that allow you to narrow down the thing you're going for, you can't do anything but hope for better luck next time.
I think the idea and main mechanism of this game is pretty neat, but the luck is too high for my taste.

RATING: 8
14-Dec-09: Played Tobago twice now. Cute game.

08-Jan-10: Played another 2 games of Tobago on Wed. We're getting better at stabbing each other now. It sort of grew on me. It started off as if this was a luck-fest with minimal options. And then someone figured out how to use the Amulet properly to chain your actions. It's a short enough game that anyone who breaks into a substantial lead tends to win. So if someone picks up 5-6 Treasures all on his own, he's going to win. So most of the games now, we collect Amulets for our killer turns, at the same time ensuring that no one else monopolises maps while vye-ing to be the first to dig Treasures up. 
It's a short game, so I'd play it as a wind-down game or as a lunch-time game or two.

24-Feb-11: Read a really good description on Tobago. "It's a one-trick pony. But it's a nifty trick."

RATING: 7.5
Fun game with unique mechanic and more depth than I expected. Nice that it's competitive without being cutthroat, at least with 2 players (haven't played more yet). Theme is stronger than most euros. Board and bits make up a large percentage of the enjoyment. Components shout "German-made quality here."  

I like this game but find myself wishing it were different:
Mechanics are backwards to the theme - you create the location of the treaure instead of finding it. Makes me wish for a game where you actually do find the treasure by deduction. Also, I admire games where the rules are simple but gameplay is deep. That's not this game; the rules seemed a little contrived and overly complicated when I first read the rulebook. But it's better than Agricola and no worse most other euros in that respect.

But the game is what it is. And what it is is good.

RATING: 6
Tobago is the biggest production yet of a "proof" game:  players use their maps scraps to hone in on a treasure and "prove" its location.  The concept is fascinating, although here it shares the limelight with a more routine game of influence.  The bits are fantastic and the method for splitting loot is game-show drama.  But Tobago is ruined by a lack of card text.  Seasoned players spend a lot of time correcting each other, even after several plays, which costs Tobago a full point.

RATING: 6.5
I got my family hooked on this one and it's a common request when I'm coming over for game night. I often forget it on purpose, however. I just don't feel like there's a whole lot of worthwhile strategy in the game. When it's my turn, I make a decision on what is best for me right then and I don't ever feel like there's an opportunity to develop or shoot for a long-term strategy. So personally, it scores low. But a few people in the family really love it. Maybe they prefer ignoring long-term strategies and enjoy making the in-the-moment decisions better.

UPDATE: The more I play this, the more I see that my initial thoughts were spot on. This game is good to get non-gamers interested and it requires them to think beyond normal board-game thinking. So it scores some gateway value. What this game does NOT deliver, is anything by way of exciting strategy. In fact, there isn't much exciting about it besides the following:

1. It's pretty. People come over and are curious to know how the board and treasure cards work together.
2. It has a sweet bidding mechanic. During the treasure distribution, the bidding is exciting and tense. I wish this was a bigger part of the overall experience.

I'm thinking about putting this up for trade.

--

UPDATE 10/5/2014

After giving this to my sister over a year ago, I finally played it again tonight. It's a good game to visit every now and then. But in the end, it was the right choice to give it up. It doesn't have much replayability, and is a bit light for my tastes. It's perfect fun for a family night though.

On comment I had is how a lot of people dislike the curse cards, but that puts more value on the Amulets, since they help to lessen the negative effects of a curse. I'm fine with them as long as I'm only playing this once a year or so. ;)

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 7
SOLD (12/12/15) --- I was very pleasantly surprised by Tobago.  I had low expectations because it was being labeled a family game/SDJ 2010 contender and a deduction game, and I generally don't like family game SDJ contenders or deduction games, so the two combined didn't sound like a game for me.  I'm glad I gave it a try though because I found it rather enjoyable.  It's certainly a light, family game that has a lot of luck/randomness.  However, it has beautiful components and the biggest thing going for it is that it felt refreshingly original.  As someone who almost always thinks that game X feels like a combination of games Y and Z, I was impressed by the fact that Tobago felt very unique, which is hard to find these days.  I look forward to playing it again soon and seeing how it holds up to repeated plays, but the modular board and variable setup make me hopeful on that front.

Received as gift: Christmas 2009

RATING: 6
This is a really interesting little game, and I like some of the mechanics quite a bit.  The components are gorgeous, and play zips along pretty quickly once everyone is up to speed.

The game's scoring system is an interesting combination of investing into various expeditions (almost like Lost Cities in spirit) and betting/risking during the unearthing of treasure.

Very clever title, and unlike most things I would normally go after.  While not my ideal cup of tea, someone who enjoys Euros and/or betting games would really enjoy this.

RATING: 7.5
Played with two, three and four and had a good time with it on all occasions. With two, the game is a little more strategic (but also a little more repetitive). The game plays more like a puzzle than its components suggest. It also taxes you more than you would think (certainly more than other family games like Thurn & Taxis and Ticket To Ride). The mechanic of playing clue cards to determine the position of the four treasures feels exciting and refreshing. Driving around to collect them is thematic and using the talismans can make for some interesting moves. Only the mechanic for dividing the treasures once they have been salvaged feels a bit too random for me, even though it does add a little suspense. Did I mention that the components of this game are nothing short of stunning? Most importantly, the game still feels original four years after its publication.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
10/2012:5
Not really impressed after my first play. Granted there were a few rules we missed, but nothing major to cripple the game. For one thing, the rules are poorly written. Looking at the rules forum section, many folks are finding the ATV movement as well as the amulet use confusing. The ATV movement just isn't intuitive and the amulet use complicates matters.

The card draw is very luck based. Several times in a row, I was unable to play any cards and it was pointless to move my ATV. Yes, I can skip my turn and swap cards, but at a huge disadvantage. A game where that happens due to no fault of mine is a MAJOR flaw in the game. It forces you to ditch cards based on the randomness of your hand with no other option. Poor rule design.

The one thing that surprised me, albeit a pleasant surprise, was that the game played quickly. We were done within the hour. That is certainly a plus for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Quite a unique experience that combines some of the deduction mechanics known from other games (such as Mr Jack) with a bit of card play and auction. I guess this would qualify as "semi-coop", but it definitely feels mostly competitive. The theme is strong, not least thanks to the great artwork and components. You do feel like you go for a treasure hunt indeed.

Just like in other deduction games, the puzzle aspect of the game is not the deduction itself (which is actually very straightforward). Rather, there is a strategic element of card play (how many clues do you add to which treasure) and area control (whoever goes and "retrieves" the treasure once it is found gets a slight advantage for how the resulting bonus points gets distributed). 

Medium-depth and length gameplay, which starts out a bit slow but quickly gets tense as things progress. High replayability, and a pleasant mix of many choices and multiple possible strategies for victory, although the only ideal move can be quite apparent at times.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
This is where the adventure begins!
\13\\13\
Chests full of gold are buried in the jungle - rumors have it that one of them is hidden next to a hut near the island’s largest mountain. Another is hidden where the roar of an untamed river can be heard, it is said. Through the fronds of a palm tree, the proud gaze of a mysteriously scrunching statue casts its spell on the unwary treasure hunter...
\13\\13\
Welcome to Tobago!
\13\\13\
The attractive, top-quality game pieces on the lovingly illustrated game board give the impression of a true mysterious treasure island. Each player contributes valuable clues, which determine where the treasures are located. The excitement mounts as the adventurers strive to be the first to arrive at the treasure sites. The 3-piece game board with its innovative design can be set up with 32 different scenarios, each providing fun and a new challenge.

RATING: 8
Really neat game. It wasn't really what I expected, nor is it particularly like any other game I've played. I like the push-your-luck mechanics of the treasures, and even of the clues ("do I play this card to put the treasure close to me, or try to grab that amulet and risk somebody else putting down the last clue and grabbing it?"). My only minor complaints are that there's not really a good way to put the random figures out and that trying to manipulate the treasure is a bit brain-burning for such an otherwise light game, but these are both minor nitpicks; this game is a lot of fun.

RATING: 7
You use cards to define where treasure is located on the map. Each card describes a condition such as next to the beach or in the mountains. Once the treasure has been identified to one spot, someone moves their token and collects it. The treasure will be made up of treasure cards numbered from 2 to 6 or a curse. The treasure allocation is interesting but can be very luck driven. I like it a lot for a family game.

Awesome components - the palm trees, stone heads, and wooden SUVs are all really cool, but it's the board that takes the cake.   The way it can be fit together in so many different combinations that all still work with the card possibilities (specifically, the "largest" areas) is really impressive.

The game itself if really fun too.  It always seems to start out slow and just when you start thinking it'll take forever to go through the stack of treasure cards, a few in a row get dug up that take out seven or eight cards each and suddenly you're flying through the deck.

Setting out the cubes can be a little bit fiddly at times, but if everyone helps out it's really not a problem at all.  A few more cubes of each color would have been nice, but chances are if you're running short it's too soon to be putting those cubes on the board anyways.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Tobago features a great and very accessible deduction mechanic in which every successive clue helps to narrow down the location of a given treasure.  This information is open to all and can be manipulated by anyone to his advantage.  The only downside to it is that as someone starts to dominate in a single entry, it becomes less and less of an incentive for others to help out.

Unfortunately, I'm not as thrilled about the board movement, the amulet as well as the assignment of the treasure cards themselves (pretty much the rest of the game).  Thus to me, Tobago is only a half decent title that plays unevenly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I was pleasantly surprised with the extremely high production value of this game!  I love deduction games and although this isn't secretive deduction, it offers a unique twist to that game mechanic.

I find the game play very enjoyable to the point where I may even say that the game is somewhat addictive!  I have yet to play a game of this complexity using such simple mechanics that plays so smoothly.

The game components are next to perfect and the game board has to be the most unique I have ever seen/played/owned!  I especially like the resin statues and hand-carved palm trees.  What attractive pieces!

I also have to mention that the box insert does an excellent job organizing all the game components with specially designed areas to hold the oddly shaped board pieces.  Two thumbs up for Rio Grande!

RATING: 4.5
BEAUTIFUL components, Rio Grande has outdone themselves.  Awesome modular board with cheerful landscape art, and awesome 3D palm trees and Easter Island heads.

And the idea of hunting treasure by DICTATING where it can be is terrific.  Fantastically original.

Unfortunately the gameplay is WAY too luck-driven for my taste.  I keep winning games based on curses randomly hitting everyone but me.  It's unsatisfying to win and frustrating to lose that way.  

And why the hell do the Easter Island heads rotate and shoot amulets onto beaches?!  Weird thematic nonsense in a game that's otherwise extremely flavorful.

RATING: 8
Interesting treasure hunt game. Players take turns to move their vehicles over the island OR play clue cards (cards that say "in a lake" or "near a palm tree" or "not on a beach") to narrow down where one of the 4 treasures might be found. Each clue played on a treasure earns the player one "grab right" for that treasure. When there's just one place left for a treasure to be, a player can move his vehicle there to pick up the treasure, earning him another "grab right". Treasures cards (numbered 2 to 6) equal to the number of grab rights are drawn from the treasure deck and distributed by a cool system.
The thing that tips for this game is that each time a treasure is found, these stone statues around the island spit out amulets all over the place. Amulets, if picked up, is basically "one extra turn". This calls for an interesting game where the choices are many. Most treasures at the end wins.
